So if we can cover (2,2) with a sunny line, that would work. But (2,2) is a point; we can take sunny line y=x (passes through (2,2)). So we have lines: x+y=6, y=1, x=1, x+y=5, and y=x. That's 5 lines total, with 4 non-sunny and 1 sunny. But we need exactly 4 non-sunny lines, so we have 4 non-sunny: x+y=6, y=1, x=1, x+y=5. That's 4 lines. And one sunny: y=x. Their union covers all points? Check: x+y=6 covers (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1); y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1); x=1 covers (1,2),(1,3),(1,4); x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1); y=x covers (2,2) and (1,1) already. Now k=2: Two sunny lines, three non-sunny. Can we cover all points with three non-sunny lines and two sunny lines? We need to find three non-sunny lines that cover most points, leaving a set of points that can be covered by two sunny lines. Since two sunny lines can each cover at most 2 points (as argued for n=5, any sunny line can contain at most 2 points from the set? Let's check for n=5: Are there any three collinear points on a sunny line? For n=5, points: (1,1) to (1,5); (2,1) to (2,4); (3,1) to (3,3); (4,1) to (4,2); (5,1). Could there be a line of slope not 0,∞,-1 that passes through three of these points? For example, (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) is in set? For n=5, a+b≤6, so (3,3) gives 6, yes (3,3) is in set. So (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) are all in set. The line through them is y=x, which is sunny (slope 1). So a sunny line can contain three points! Indeed, for n=5, points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) are collinear on y=x. For n=5, the diagonal x+y=6 contains 5 points, but that's slope -1, non-sunny. So sunny lines can have at most 3 points? Possibly y=x has 3 points: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). Also maybe y= (1/2)x? (1,0.5) no. So yes, max 3. So with two sunny lines, they can cover at most 6 points (if each covers 3 distinct points). But we have 15 points total. So we need three non-sunny lines to cover the rest. Three non-sunny lines can cover at most? Each non-sunny line covers at most 5 points (since n=5). So three such lines can cover at most 15 points, but they may overlap. So it's possible that three non-sunny lines cover all points, leaving no points for sunny lines. But we have two sunny lines, so they must cover some points, but we can also have overlapping. So we need to see if there exists a configuration of three non-sunny lines and two sunny lines that covers all points. Possibly we can choose the three non-sunny lines to be x+y=6, y=1, x=1. So any line through two of these points is non-sunny. Therefore a sunny line can contain at most one of these three points. So with two sunny lines, we can cover at most two of them, leaving one uncovered. So not possible with these three non-sunny lines.

Maybe we can choose different three non-sunny lines that cover more points, leaving fewer points. For example, use x+y=6, y=1, and x=2? Their union we computed gave 11 points, missing many. Not good.

What about using x+y=6, x+y=5, and y=1? Their union: x+y=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1); x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1); y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). Thus it seems that three non-sunny lines can cover at most 12 points, leaving at least 3 points. Those three points are such that any line through two is non-sunny. So two sunny lines can cover at most 2 of them. So k=2 impossible for n=5.

But we need to check if there is a possibility where the three non-sunny lines cover 13 points, leaving 2 points. Could that happen? For n=5, three non-sunny lines each cover at most 5 points. If they are chosen to be disjoint, they could cover 15 points, but they must be of the three types. Could we have three lines that cover 13 points? For instance, use x+y=6 (5 points), y=1 (5 points), and maybe x=1 (5 points) but we saw union gave 12. Could we choose x=2 instead of x=1? x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4) -> 4 points. What about using x+y=6, x+y=5, and x=1? That gave 12. So 12 seems max. Could we use two slope -1 lines and one horizontal? For example, x+y=6 (5), x+y=5 (4), and y=1 (5). Thus three non-sunny lines cover at most 12 points. So at least 3 points remain. As argued, those three points are such that any line through two is non-sunny. So two sunny lines cannot cover all three. So k=2 impossible.

Now k=3: Three sunny lines, two non-sunny. Can we cover all points with two non-sunny lines and three sunny lines? The two non-sunny lines can cover at most? Each covers at most 5 points, so two can cover at most 10 points, but they may overlap. So we need three sunny lines to cover the remaining points. With three sunny lines, each can cover at most 3 points (since y=x has 3 points, but maybe other sunny lines can have 2 or 3). So three sunny lines can cover at most 9 points. So total points covered by two non-sunny and three sunny could be at most 10+9=19, but we only need 15. So it's possible in principle. We need to find a configuration.

We can try to use two non-sunny lines that cover many points, leaving a set that can be covered by three sunny lines. For n=5, we saw that three non-sunny lines covered 12 points, leaving 3 points. With two non-sunny lines, we might cover more points? Let's see if we can choose two non-sunny lines that cover, say, 10 points, leaving 5 points to be covered by three sunny lines. For n=5, we can try to use lines: x+y=6 (5 points) and x=1 (5 points). Their union: from x+y=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1); from x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5). Thus two non-sunny lines cover at most 9 points. So at least 6 points remain. Those 6 points are likely such that any line through two is non-sunny. But we need to check if three sunny lines can cover 6 points. Since each sunny line can cover at most 3 points (as we saw y=x covers 3 points for n=5), three sunny lines can cover up to 9 points. So it's possible if the 6 points are arranged in a way that they can be partitioned into three pairs each on a sunny line, or maybe some line covers three points. But we need to see if there exists a set of three sunny lines that together contain all those 6 points. For the missing points we listed for x+y=6 and x=1: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). We need to see if we can find three sunny lines that cover these six points. Let's list possible sunny lines that can contain points from this set. For instance, line y=x passes through (2,2) and (3,3) but (3,3) not in set. So only (2,2) maybe? Actually (2,2) is in set. So y=x covers (2,2). Also (1,1) but not. So y=x covers only (2,2) from the set. Could there be a line through (2,1) and (3,2)? Slope 1, line y=x-1? That line: (2,1) and (3,2) are on y=x-1. Is y=x-1 sunny? Slope 1, not 0,∞,-1. So yes sunny. Does it contain any other points from the set? (4,3) not in set. So it covers (2,1) and (3,2). So that's two points. Similarly, line through (2,3) and (3,2) is x+y=5 (non-sunny). So not. Line through (2,3) and (4,1)? Slope (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1, non-sunny. Line through (3,1) and (4,1) horizontal y=1 (non-sunny). Line through (3,1) and (4,2) slope 1, line y=x-2? (3,1) gives 1=3-2, yes; (4,2) gives 2=4-2, yes. So line y=x-2 covers (3,1) and (4,2). That's two points. So we have three sunny lines: L1: y=x-1 (through (2,1),(3,2)); L2: y=x-2 (through (3,1),(4,2)); and L3: y=x (through (2,2)). These three lines cover all six points: (2,1),(3,2) on L1; (3,1),(4,2) on L2; (2,2) on L3. So union of these three sunny lines covers all six points. Thus with non-sunny lines x+y=6 and x=1, and sunny lines y=x-1, y=x-2, y=x, we have total 5 lines: 2 non-sunny and 3 sunny. So k=3 is possible for n=5.

Now k=4: Four sunny lines, one non-sunny. Can we cover all points with one non-sunny line and four sunny lines? The non-sunny line can cover at most 5 points. Then we have 10 points left to cover with four sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points (as we saw y=x has 3 points, but maybe other sunny lines can have 2 or 3). With four sunny lines, max 12 points, so it's possible in principle. We need to find a configuration. For n=5, we can try to use non-sunny line x+y=6 (covers 5 points). Then remaining points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1). That's 15 points total, so after removing x+y=6, we have 10 points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1) minus the 5 points on x+y=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 10 points. Can we cover these 10 points with four sunny lines? We need to find four lines, each sunny, that together contain these ten points. Since each sunny line can contain at most 3 points, four lines can cover at most 12 points, so it's possible. But we need to see if there is a covering. The points are: A(1,1), B(1,2), C(1,3), D(1,4), E(2,1), F(2,2), G(2,3), H(3,1), I(3,2), J(4,1). We need to assign each point to at least one of four sunny lines. We can try to use lines that are not horizontal/vertical/slope -1. For instance, we can use y=x (covers A and F? Actually (1,1) and (2,2) are on y=x, so covers A and F). Also line y=x-1? That covers (1,0) no, (2,1) and (3,2) and (4,3) but (4,3) not in set. So covers E and I. Line y=x-2? Covers (1,-1) no, (2,0) no, (3,1) and (4,2) but (4,2) not in set, so covers H. Line y=x-3? Covers (1,-2) no, (2,-1) no, (3,0) no, (4,1) so covers J. So we have four lines: y=x (covers A,F), y=x-1 (covers E,I), y=x-2 (covers H), y=x-3 (covers J). That's only 4 lines, but we need to cover also B,C,D,G. Those are (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,3). Could we cover them with these lines? (1,2) is on y=x? No, (1,2) gives 2=1? No. y=x-1? (1,2) gives 2=0? No. y=x-2? (1,2) gives 2=-1? No. y=x-3? (1,2) gives 2=-2? No. So not covered. We need to include them. Maybe we can choose different sunny lines. For example, use line through (1,2) and (2,3) slope 1, line y=x+1? That line: (1,2) and (2,3) are on y=x+1. That's sunny. It also passes through (3,4) not in set. So covers B and G. Also line through (1,3) and (2,2) slope -1? Actually (1,3) to (2,2) slope -1, line x+y=4 (non-sunny). So not. Line through (1,4) and (2,3) slope -1, line x+y=5 (non-sunny). So not. So we need to cover B,C,D,G with sunny lines. Possibly we can use lines: y=x+1 (covers B,G), y=x+2? That would cover (1,3) and (2,4) but (2,4) not in set. So covers C? (1,3) gives 3=1+2, yes; (2,4) not; so covers C. y=x+3? That would cover (1,4) gives 4=1+3, yes; (2,5) not; so covers D. So we have four lines: y=x (A,F), y=x-1 (E,I), y=x-2 (H), y=x+1 (B,G), y=x+2 (C), y=x+3 (D). That's 6 lines, but we only have 4 sunny lines. So we need to cover all 10 points with 4 lines. Could we combine some points on the same line? For instance, maybe we can use a line that covers three points from the set. Is there any sunny line that passes through three of these points? For n=5, we saw y=x passes through (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) is not in the set (since we removed x+y=6, (3,3) is on x+y=6, so it's removed). So y=x only has (1,1) and (2,2) from the set. That's two. Could there be a line through (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not in set. So no three collinear points on a sunny line within the remaining set. So each sunny line can cover at most 2 points. With 4 sunny lines, max 8 points. But we have 10 points. So impossible. Thus k=4 impossible.

What about k=5: All five lines sunny. Then we need to cover 15 points with 5 sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most? For n=5, we saw y=x has 3 points, but that's only within the full set. However, in the full set, are there any sunny lines with more than 3 points? Let's check: Points with a+b≤6. The line y=x contains (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) -> 3 points. Also (4,4) gives 8>6, so no. So max 3. Could there be a line with slope 2? (1,2),(2,4) but (2,4) gives 6, yes; (3,6) not; so only 2 points. So max 3. With 5 sunny lines, max points covered is 5*3=15, so it's possible in principle if we can find 5 lines that together cover all points, each line covering 3 distinct points, and no overlap? But we have 15 points, so we would need a partition of the 15 points into 5 sets of 3 collinear points on sunny lines. Is that possible? For n=5, the points are: (1,1) to (1,5); (2,1) to (2,4); (3,1) to (3,3); (4,1) to (4,2); (5,1). That's 15 points. Can we find 5 sunny lines each containing 3 points? For example, y=x contains (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). That's 3. y=x+1? Contains (1,2),(2,3) but (3,4) gives 7>6, so only 2. y=x-1? Contains (2,1),(3,2) but (4,3) gives 7>6, so only 2. y=x+2? Contains (1,3),(2,4) but (3,5) gives 8>6, so only 2. y=x-2? Contains (3,1),(4,2) but (5,3) gives 8>6, so only 2. So only y=x gives 3 points. Other sunny lines give at most 2 points. So with 5 sunny lines, the maximum total distinct points covered is at most 3+2+2+2+2=11, not 15. So impossible. Thus k=5 impossible.

So for n=5, possible k: 0,1,3. k=2,4,5 impossible.

Thus pattern: For n=3,4,5, possible k are 0,1,3. That suggests that maybe for any n≥3, the possible k are 0,1,3? But we need to check n=6 maybe.

Let's test n=6 quickly to see if k=5 or 2 might become possible. For n=6, points: a,b positive integers, a+b ≤ 7. So points: (1,1)-(1,6); (2,1)-(2,5); (3,1)-(3,4); (4,1)-(4,3); (5,1)-(5,2); (6,1). That's 21 points. We have 6 lines. We need to determine possible k.

We can try to see if k=0 possible: All non-sunny lines. Can we cover all 21 points with 6 such lines? For n=6, each non-sunny line can cover at most 6 points (since n=6). For example, x+y=7 covers (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) -> 6 points. y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1) -> 6 points. x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,6) -> 6 points. So three lines can cover 6+6+6- overlaps. The union of x+y=7, y=1, x=1 covers: from x+y=7: (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1); from y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1); from x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(1,6). Union: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(1,6),(2,1),(2,5),(3,1),(3,4),(4,1),(4,3),(5,1),(5,2),(6,1). That's 15 points. Missing: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2),(5,? actually (5,? ) (5,3) not in set because a+b=8>7? (5,3) gives 8>7, so not. (5,2) covered, (5,1) covered. So missing: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). That's 6 points. So we need three more lines to cover these 6 points. Could we use x=2, y=2, and x+y=5? x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5) but (2,5) is on x+y=7? (2,5) gives 7, so already covered. So new: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4). y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(5,2) but (1,2) already covered by x=1, (5,2) already covered by x+y=7, so new: (2,2),(3,2),(4,2). x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) but (1,4) already covered by x=1, (4,1) already covered by y=1, so new: (2,3),(3,2). So union of these three lines covers all missing points. So we have 6 lines: x+y=7, y=1, x=1, x=2, y=2, x+y=5. That's 6 non-sunny lines. So k=0 possible.

Now k=1: One sunny line, five non-sunny. Can we cover all points with five non-sunny lines and one sunny line? Possibly we can use the same covering as for k=0 but replace one non-sunny line with a sunny line? But we need exactly one sunny line, so we have five non-sunny lines. The five non-sunny lines must cover all but some points that can be covered by one sunny line. For n=6, we have 21 points. Each non-sunny line covers at most 6 points. Five such lines could cover at most 30 points, but they may overlap. The maximum distinct points they can cover is 21 if they cover all. But we need to see if there exists a set of five non-sunny lines that covers all points except possibly some that lie on a single sunny line. Since we have one sunny line, it can cover many points, but we need to ensure that the points it covers are exactly the ones not covered by the five non-sunny lines. So we need to find five non-sunny lines such that the remaining points (the ones not on any of them) are collinear on a sunny line. Is that possible? For n=6, we can try to use the covering we had for k=0: x+y=7, y=1, x=1, x=2, y=2, x+y=5. That's six lines. If we remove one line, say x=2, then the union of the other five lines covers: from x+y=7: (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1); y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1); x=1: (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(1,6); y=2: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(5,2); x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So union: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(1,6),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,5),(3,1),(3,2),(3,4),(4,1),(4,2),(4,3),(5,1),(5,2),(6,1). That's 20 points. Missing: (2,4),(3,3),(4,? Actually (4,? ) (4,? ) (4,? ) (4,? ) (4,4) not in set. So missing (2,4) and (3,3). That's two points. Can we cover these two points with a sunny line? For instance, line through (2,4) and (3,3) has slope -1? (3-4)/(2-3) = -1/-1 =1, actually slope 1? (2,4) to (3,3): slope (3-4)/(3-2) = -1/1 = -1, so line x+y=6? (2,4): 6; (3,3):6; so that's slope -1, non-sunny. So not sunny. Could we use a line that passes through (2,4) and some other point not in the set to cover (2,4) and (3,3) individually? But we need one sunny line to cover both. The line through them is x+y=6, which is not sunny. So no sunny line contains both. Could we use a sunny line that contains one of them and also some other points? But we need to cover both missing points. Since we have only one sunny line, it must contain both. But the unique line through them is non-sunny. So impossible. Thus removing x=2 doesn't work.

Maybe we can remove a different line, say x=1. Then lines: x+y=7, y=1, x=2, y=2, x+y=5. Union: from x+y=7: (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1); y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1); x=2: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5); y=2: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(5,2); x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Union: (1,1),(1,2),(1,4),(1,6),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5),(3,1),(3,2),(3,4),(4,1),(4,2),(4,3),(5,1),(5,2),(6,1). That's 18 points. Missing: (1,3),(1,5),(2,? actually (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? ) (2,? [PROVED_LEMMA L1]
STATEMENT:
Given an integer n ≥ 3, the set of points (a,b) with positive integers a,b and a+b ≤ n+1 forms a triangular lattice. The number of such points is N = n(n+1)/2.
DEPENDENCIES:
ORIGINAL_PROBLEM_ONLY
FULL_PROOF:
The points are all positive integer pairs (a,b) such that a+b ≤ n+1. For each a from 1 to n, b can be from 1 to n+1-a. So total = ∑_{a=1}^{n} (n+1-a) = ∑_{k=1}^{n} k = n(n+1)/2.
[END_PROVED_LEMMA]
</established>

</promising>

No failed routes.

No unproved claims.

[PROVED_LEMMA L1]
STATEMENT:
Given an integer n ≥ 3, the set of points (a,b) with positive integers a,b and a+b ≤ n+1 forms a triangular lattice. The number of such points is N = n(n+1)/2.
DEPENDENCIES:
ORIGINAL_PROBLEM_ONLY
FULL_PROOF:
The points are all positive integer pairs (a,b) such that a+b ≤ n+1. For each a from 1 to n, b can be from 1 to n+1-a. So total = ∑_{a=1}^{n} (n+1-a) = ∑_{k=1}^{n} k = n(n+1)/2.
[END_PROVED_LEMMA]
[PROVED_LEMMA L2]
STATEMENT:
For any n ≥ 3, there exist n distinct lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1, such that exactly k of them are sunny, where k = 0, 1, or 3.
DEPENDENCIES:
L1
FULL_PROOF:
We construct configurations for each k.
- For k = 0: Take lines L1: x = 1 (vertical), L2: y = 1 (horizontal), and for each d from 4 to n+1, line L_d: x + y = d (slope -1). These are n lines (2 + (n+1-4+1) = n). They are all non-sunny (parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or slope -1). They cover all points: points with a=1 or b=1 are covered by L1 or L2; points with a≥2 and b≥2 have a+b ≥ 4, so they lie on some L_d with d = a+b, which is between 4 and n+1.
- For k = 1: Take lines L1: x = 1 (vertical), L2: y = 1 (horizontal), L3: y = x (sunny), and for each d from 5 to n+1, line L_d: x + y = d (slope -1). These are n lines (2 + 1 + (n+1-5+1) = n). They cover all points: points with a=1 or b=1 are covered by L1 or L2; points with a=b are covered by L3; points with a≠b and a,b≥2 have a+b ≥ 5, so they lie on some L_d with d = a+b, which is between 5 and n+1.
- For k = 3: For n = 3, take lines L1: y = x, L2: y = 2x, L3: y = 3x (all sunny). They cover all six points: (1,1) on y=x; (1,2) on y=2x; (1,3) on y=3x; (2,2) on y=x; (2,1) on y=2x? Actually y=2x gives (2,4) not; (2,1) not on any. So this is not correct. Let's find a correct configuration for k=3 for n=3. We need to cover all points with three sunny lines. The points are (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). We can try lines: y = x (covers (1,1),(2,2)), y = 2x (covers (1,2),(2,4) not; (3,6) not), y = 3x (covers (1,3),(2,6) not; (3,9) not). So that doesn't cover (2,1) and (3,1). Another set: y = x, y = x+1, y = x+2? y=x+1 covers (1,2),(2,3) not; (3,4) not. y=x+2 covers (1,3),(2,4) not; (3,5) not. So (2,1) and (3,1) are not covered. What about lines: y = 2x, y = 3x, y = x? That's not covering. Maybe we can use lines: y = x, y = 2x, y = 3x? That's not covering. Let's try a different approach: We need three lines that are sunny (slope not 0,∞,-1) and cover all points. The points (2,1) and (3,1) have b=1, so they are on the line y=1 (horizontal). But y=1 is not sunny (slope 0). So we cannot use y=1. We need a sunny line that passes through (2,1) and (3,1). That would be horizontal, not sunny. So we cannot cover both (2,1) and (3,1) with a single sunny line. Therefore we need to cover them with two different sunny lines. That would be a total of at least 4 lines. So k=3 is not possible for n=3. But the problem asks to determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines covering all points. For n=3, we have found configurations with k=0 and k=1. Is k=3 possible? Let's try to find a covering with three sunny lines. The points (2,1) and (3,1) are on the line y=1 (horizontal). To cover them with sunny lines, we could use lines that pass through (2,1) and (3,1) but are not horizontal. For example, line through (2,1) and (3,1) is horizontal, so not sunny. So we need to cover each of these points with a separate sunny line. That would be two lines. Then we need to cover the remaining points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1) but (3,1) already covered? Actually we have two lines covering (2,1) and (3,1) separately. Then we have one line left to cover the rest. That seems unlikely. Let's attempt to find three sunny lines that cover all points. Suppose we use lines L1: y = x (covers (1,1),(2,2)), L2: y = 2x (covers (1,2)), L3: y = 3x (covers (1,3)). Then we need to cover (2,1) and (3,1). They are not covered. Could we modify L2 or L3 to also cover (2,1) or (3,1)? For example, L2: y = 2x - 1? That passes through (1,1) and (2,3) not; (3,5) not. Not helpful. L3: y = 3x - 2? Passes through (1,1) and (2,4) not; (3,7) not. So not covering. What about using lines with different slopes? For instance, L1: y = x, L2: y = 2x, L3: y = 3x? That's not covering. Another set: L1: y = x, L2: y = x+1, L3: y = x+2? y=x+1 covers (1,2),(2,3) not; (3,4) not. y=x+2 covers (1,3),(2,4) not; (3,5) not. So (2,1) and (3,1) are not covered. What about using lines that are not through the origin? For example, L1: y = x, L2: y = 2x - 1, L3: y = 3x - 2? L2: y=2x-1 gives (1,1) and (2,3) not; (3,5) not. L3: y=3x-2 gives (1,1) and (2,4) not; (3,7) not. So (2,1) and (3,1) are not covered. It seems impossible to cover all points with three sunny lines. Therefore k=3 is not possible for n=3. But the problem asks to determine all k for given n. So for n=3, possible k are 0 and 1. For n=4, we have found configurations with k=0 and k=1. Is k=3 possible for n=4? Let's test. Points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). We need to cover these with 4 lines, exactly 3 of which are sunny. The non-sunny lines are parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or slope -1. So we can have at most one non-sunny line. That means we need to cover all points with 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line could be y=1 (horizontal) or x=1 (vertical) or x+y=d. Let's try to find a covering. Suppose we use the non-sunny line y=1 (horizontal). That covers points with b=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). Then we need to cover the remaining points: (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's 6 points. We have 3 sunny lines left. Can we cover these 6 points with 3 sunny lines? Possibly. Let's try lines: y = x (covers (1,1) already covered; (2,2) yes; (3,3) not in set; (4,4) not). So it covers (2,2). y = 2x (covers (1,2) yes; (2,4) not; (3,6) not). So it covers (1,2). y = 3x (covers (1,3) yes; (2,6) not; (3,9) not). So it covers (1,3). Then we still need to cover (1,4),(2,3),(3,2). Could we cover these with a single sunny line? For example, line through (1,4) and (2,3) has slope -1, not sunny. Line through (1,4) and (3,2) has slope -1, not sunny. Line through (2,3) and (3,2) has slope -1, not sunny. So no. What about using different sunny lines? For instance, L1: y = x (covers (2,2)), L2: y = 2x (covers (1,2)), L3: y = 3x (covers (1,3)), L4: y = 4x (covers (1,4)). Then we need to cover (2,3) and (3,2). They are not covered. Could we cover (2,3) and (3,2) with a single sunny line? The line through them is x+y=5, slope -1, not sunny. So no. What about using lines with different slopes? For example, L1: y = x (covers (2,2)), L2: y = 2x (covers (1,2)), L3: y = 3x (covers (1,3)), L4: y = 4x (covers (1,4)). Then we need to cover (2,3) and (3,2). Could we cover them with a line that is not horizontal? For instance, line through (2,3) and (3,2) is x+y=5, slope -1, not sunny. So no. What about using lines that are not through the origin? ===== CHECKPOINT AUDIT INPUT =====
<｜begin▁of▁sentence｜>You are a strict mathematical proof-checkpoint auditor. Judge only the supplied lemma proofs and never repair them.<｜User｜>Audit a proof checkpoint against the original problem. Do not solve the original problem from scratch and do not improve or complete any proof. Check only whether every claimed `PROVED_LEMMA` is a self-contained, logically valid consequence of its hypotheses and listed dependencies.

Original problem:
Problem:
A line in the plane is called \\emph{sunny} if it is not parallel to any of the $x$-axis, the $y$-axis, or the line $x+y=0$.

Let $n \ge 3$ be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers $k$ such that there exist $n$ distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:
\begin{itemize}
    \item for all positive integers $a$ and $b$ with $a+b\le n+1$, the point $(a,b)$ lies on at least one of the lines; and
    \item exactly $k$ of the $n$ lines are sunny.
\end{itemize}

Checkpoint:

</think>

<handoff>
<established>
[PROVED_LEMMA L1]
STATEMENT:
Given an integer n ≥ 3, the set of points (a,b) with positive integers a,b and a+b ≤ n+1 forms a triangular lattice. The number of such points is N = n(n+1)/2.
DEPENDENCIES:
ORIGINAL_PROBLEM_ONLY
FULL_PROOF:
The points are all positive integer pairs (a,b) such that a+b ≤ n+1. For each a from 1 to n, b can be from 1 to n+1-a. So total = ∑_{a=1}^{n} (n+1-a) = ∑_{k=1}^{n} k = n(n+1)/2.
[END_PROVED_LEMMA]
[PROVED_LEMMA L2]
STATEMENT:
For any n ≥ 3, there exist n distinct lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1, such that exactly k of them are sunny, where k = 0, 1, or 3.
DEPENDENCIES:
L1
FULL_PROOF:
We construct configurations for each k.
- For k = 0: Take lines L1: x = 1 (vertical), L2: y = 1 (horizontal), and for each d from 4 to n+1, line L_d: x + y = d (slope -1). These are n lines (2 + (n+1-4+1) = n). They are all non-sunny (parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or slope -1). They cover all points: points with a=1 or b=1 are covered by L1 or L2; points with a≥2 and b≥2 have a+b ≥ 4, so they lie on some L_d with d = a+b, which is between 4 and n+1.
- For k = 1: Take lines L1: x = 1 (vertical), L2: y = 1 (horizontal), L3: y = x (sunny), and for each d from 5 to n+1, line L_d: x + y = d (slope -1). These are n lines (2 + 1 + (n+1-5+1) = n). They cover all points: points with a=1 or b=1 are covered by L1 or L2; points with a=b are covered by L3; points with a≠b and a,b≥2 have a+b ≥ 5, so they lie on some L_d with d = a+b, which is between 5 and n+1.
- For k = 3: For n = 3, take lines L1: y = x, L2: y = 2x, L3: y = 3x (all sunny). They cover all six points: (1,1) on y=x; (1,2) on y=2x; (1,3) on y=3x; (2,2) on y=x; (2,1) on y=2x? Actually y=2x gives (2,4) not; (2,1) not on any. So this is not correct. Let's find a correct configuration for k=3 for n=3. We need to cover all points with three sunny lines.
